10.1016/j.jmp.2023.102781
The aim of the present article is to lay out the foundations of an axiomatic theory of attribute maps. These last can be regarded as an “upgrade” of skill maps ( Düntsch & Gediga, 1995 ) to the case of polytomous items and dichotomous skills. The theory developed here can be regarded as an extension of the so-called competence-based knowledge structure theory (CbKST;  Doignon, 1994; Düntsch & Gediga, 1995; Falmagne, Koppen, Villano, Doignon, & Johanessen, 1990; Gediga & Düntsch, 2002; Heller, Augustin, Hockemeyer, Stefanutti, & Albert, 2013; Heller, Stefanutti, Anselmi, & Robusto, 2015; Heller, Ünlü, & Albert, 2013; Korossy, 1997, 1999 ).

When it was initially formulated in 1985, knowledge space theory (KST;  Doignon & Falmagne, 1985, 1999; Falmagne & Doignon, 2011; Falmagne et al., 1990 ) was aimed at non-numerically assessing the knowledge of individuals through a set of dichotomous items. In this framework, the  knowledge state  of an individual was intended as the specific set of items about a certain knowledge domain that an individual masters. Both a deterministic and a probabilistic framework were developed to this aim. The former was used to establish the properties of specific kinds of collections of knowledge states, called  knowledge structures , and to provide methods for building such structures. Different properties of knowledge structures (e.g., closure under set union of the collection of states) were used to define different kinds of structures (e.g.,  knowledge spaces ), each of which presented specific interpretations from the learning perspective (e.g., the mastering of an item can be achieved through different learning paths). From a probabilistic point of view, the basic local independence model (BLIM;  Falmagne & Doignon, 1988a, 1988b ) was developed to provide the deterministic part of the theory with a probabilistic reference model. Parameters of this model are, for each item,  lucky guess  and  careless error  probabilities, and the probability of each knowledge state in the population. The deterministic and probabilistic components of KST lead to the development and implementation of adaptive assessment algorithms used to discover the knowledge state of individuals. Some examples of the practical implementation of these algorithms are the ALEKS ( www.aleks.com , see e.g.,  Falmagne, Albert, Doble, Eppstein, & Hu, 2013 ) and the StatKnowlab ( Anselmi, Stefanutti, de Chiusole, & Robusto, 2021; de Chiusole, Stefanutti, Anselmi, & Robusto, 2020 ) platforms.

The study of the cognitive processes or abilities implied in items’ solution was not considered in the first phase of KST development. Later on, starting from the beginning of the 90s, the study and formal definition of the connections between the  skills  needed to solve the items and the observed answering behavior of individuals lead to the first definition of the CbKST. This cognitive development of the theory introduced the distinction between the knowledge state at the performance level, intended as the set of items mastered by an individual, and the  competence state , intended as the set of skills that the individual must possess to solve a set of items. The mapping that assigns to each item the set of skills needed to solve it is the  skill map . In CbKST, the link between the skills and the solution of each item is usually modeled with the  conjunctive model , the  disjunctive model , or the  competency model . In the conjunctive model all the skills assigned to an item must be possessed by an individual to solve it; in the disjunctive model each one of the skills assigned to the item is sufficient for an individual to solve it; finally, in the competency model an item can be solved using one of different sets of skills. Starting from the skill map and via one of the listed models it is possible to delineate the knowledge states existing in the population. The methods of construction of a skill map include both theory-driven ( Düntsch & Gediga, 1995 ) and data-driven ( de Chiusole, Spoto, & Stefanutti, 2020; de Chiusole, Stefanutti, & Spoto, 2017; Spoto, Stefanutti, & Vidotto, 2016 ) procedures.

What remained unchanged between KST and CbKST is the dichotomous nature of both items and skills: each item solution is dichotomously classified as “right” or “wrong”, each skill can be possessed or not by an individual, and each skill can be needed or not to solve an item.

Dichotomous items are well suited for the aim of assessing knowledge. Nonetheless, the possibility of extending KST beyond the dichotomous case has been studied since the paper by  Schrepp (1997) . Polytomous items offer the possibility of not only enriching the assessment of knowledge, but also extending the KST approach to other fields, such as psychological, personality, and attitudes assessment. Despite the potential importance of this extension, after the work by Schrepp many years passed before some further proposals were advanced in this direction.

The first contribution was proposed by  Bartl and Belohlavek (2011) , and further developed by  Zhou, Li, Wang, and Sun (2022) . Both these contributions referred to the application of  fuzzy set theory  to the definition of knowledge structures in which the items had a non dichotomous answering format.

The other contributions were more adherent to the classical KST approach and to the work by Schrepp.  Heller (2021)  proposed a polytomous extension of the deterministic part of the theory and of the structure construction methods by showing how the procedures adopted in dichotomous KST can be generalized to the polytomous case. Along the lines defined by  Schrepp (1997) , and in even more strict connection to Schrepp’s work than Heller, the polytomous extension of the deterministic components of KST was proposed by  Stefanutti, Anselmi, de Chiusole, and Spoto (2020) . This last work led also to the generalization of the probabilistic framework of KST by means of the polytomous local independence model (PoLIM;  Stefanutti, de Chiusole, Anselmi, & Spoto, 2020 ), a model that addresses items with more than two answer alternatives.

Moreover,  Stefanutti, de Chiusole, Gondan, and Maurer (2020)  proposed both a deterministic and a probabilistic framework for the polytomous extension of KST aimed at modeling  misconceptions . This extension appears to be particularly useful when knowledge is assessed, since it allows to model the different kinds of “wrong” answers and relate them to particular bugs in the learning process of the individual.

In sum, while the polytomous extension of KST has been addressed from both the deterministic and probabilistic perspectives (even if many points, such as model identifiability, remain to be studied yet), the polytomous extension of CbKST has not been sufficiently explored yet. The investigation in this field appears of great relevance for future applications of KST to many different research fields.

In order to contribute to the progress in the study of the delineation of a polytomous structure from a skill map, the present article aims at detecting and formalizing a set of axioms and conditions that ensure the logical coherence and the compatibility between the skill assignment defined by the skill map and the possibly delineated polytomous structure.

The article is structured as follows. Section  2  provides the necessary backgrounds about CbKST and a polytomous extension of KST. The main contribution of the article is found in Section  3 , where an extension of CbKST to polytomous items is developed on the basis of a set of four axioms (necessary and sufficient conditions) for consistently deriving a polytomous performance structure from a set of potentially non-independent attributes by a conjunctive model. Special compatibility conditions arise between the “attribute map” (an extension of the notion of a skill map to polytomous items) and the underlying structure on the set of attributes, which are illustrated and discussed in the section. A variety of examples of application of the theory are offered in Section  4 . Various cases are covered in the section, including a “quasi-nominal case”, a “partial order case”, and a “linear order case”. Section  5  provides some final remarks and closes the article.

This extension assumes that the response scale is the same across all items. Confining consideration to finite sets of items and response values,  Heller (2021)  further generalizes polytomous KST to item-specific response scales.

Another polytomous approach was proposed by  Stefanutti, de Chiusole, Gondan, and Maurer (2020)  with the aim of modeling misconceptions in CbKST. Under this approach, for every

In this section the theory of attribute structures is developed. An attribute structure generalizes the dichotomous notion of a skill map to polytomous items and dichotomous skills.

Condition (P1) states that every item has at least two admissible response values (i.e., the item is at least dichotomous). By Condition (P2), every response value is admissible for at least one item. It should be noted that admissibility and “correctness” are distinct concepts. In the assessment of knowledge or skills, the response to an item may be admissible and not correct, whereas the contrary cannot occur. In psychological assessment, questionnaires are often made of items without any “correct” response. However, only a well defined set of responses are admissible for each of the items (e.g., a degree of agreement).

The concept of an admissibility relation allows to slightly generalize the notion of a polytomous state defined in Section  2 , and provided by  Stefanutti, Anselmi, et al. (2020)  and  Heller (2021) .

Also the notion of a polytomous structure undergoes a slight generalization, compared to the one given by, e.g.,  Heller (2021) .

Definition 3 
                            A  polytomous structure  is a 4-tuple  
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     Q 
                                     , 
                                     V 
                                     , 
                                     P 
                                     , 
                                     K 
                                     ) 
                                  
                                where:  
                                  Q 
                                is a set of items,  
                                  V 
                                is a set of response values,  
                                  P 
                                is an admissibility relation for  
                                  Q 
                                and  
                                  V 
                               , and  
                                  
                                     K 
                                     ⊆ 
                                     P 
                                  
                                is a collection of polytomous states  
                                  
                                     K 
                                     : 
                                     Q 
                                     → 
                                     V 
                                  
                               , that contains a unique minimal element  
                                  ⊥ 
                                with respect to  
                                  ⊑ 
                               , and is such that  
                                  
                                     ⋃ 
                                     K 
                                     = 
                                     P 
                                  
                               .

A  polytomous structure  is a 4-tuple  
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     Q 
                                     , 
                                     V 
                                     , 
                                     P 
                                     , 
                                     K 
                                     ) 
                                  
                                where:  
                                  Q 
                                is a set of items,  
                                  V 
                                is a set of response values,  
                                  P 
                                is an admissibility relation for  
                                  Q 
                                and  
                                  V 
                               , and  
                                  
                                     K 
                                     ⊆ 
                                     P 
                                  
                                is a collection of polytomous states  
                                  
                                     K 
                                     : 
                                     Q 
                                     → 
                                     V 
                                  
                               , that contains a unique minimal element  
                                  ⊥ 
                                with respect to  
                                  ⊑

                                     ⋃ 
                                     K 
                                     = 
                                     P 
                                  
                               .

The generalization considered here admits that the polytomous structure misses a top element. This is in line with recent developments of KST like, e.g.,  Stefanutti, de Chiusole, Gondan, and Maurer (2020) . Furthermore, it is consistent with the idea that not all item–response pairs are admissible. Thus, the union of all the states in  
                            K 
                          is just the admissibility relation, rather then being the full Cartesian product  
                            
                               Q 
                               × 
                               V 
                            
                         .

In this section, the notion of a skill map is generalized to polytomous items and dichotomous skills. Since the resulting generalized concept is, in principle, applicable outside the standard educational area of KST, its name does not contain the term “skill”. Instead, this last is replaced by the more neutral term “attribute”, and this substitution yields the name of an “attribute map”, which is defined as follows. Let

The aim of the attribute map  
                            τ 
                          is to provide a set of rules for establishing, in a deterministic manner, what admissible response will be provided to an item  
                            q 
                          by an individual. Later, in this section, it is shown that the set of Axioms (A1) to (A4) of an attribute map represents a set of necessary and sufficient conditions for delineating a polytomous structure in a “conjunctive” sense. Axiom (A1) guarantees the existence of a “least” response value  
                            z 
                          in  
                            V 
                          which is admissible for every item in  
                            Q 
                         . The mapping  
                            τ 
                          assigns no attributes to the pair  
                            
                               ( 
                               q 
                               , 
                               z 
                               ) 
                            
                         . The interpretation of  
                            z 
                          depends on the context. It may be regarded as the “default” response value that is provided to an item when no other response values, admissible for  
                            q 
                         , are “appropriate” according to the individual. To give some examples, in a partial credit item, the response  
                            z 
                          could represent the “totally wrong” answer; in expressing a degree of agreement with an item,  
                            z 
                          may represent the “neutral” (neither agreement, nor disagreement) level of the scale. A scale for judging “pleasantness of a service” could go from extremely pleasant to extremely unpleasant, passing by the “neutral” point  
                            z 
                         . In judging how often a given behavior is exhibited by a patient,  
                            z 
                          could represent the “never” point of the rating scale.

Axiom (A2) is an order preserving condition. If two response values  
                            v 
                          and  
                            w 
                          are both admissible for an item  
                            q 
                          and the latter requires all the attributes required by the former plus, eventually, some other, then the former must precede the latter in the order  
                            ≼ 
                         . The reverse condition is not required. Thus, there may be response values  
                            
                               v 
                               , 
                               w 
                               ∈ 
                               V 
                            
                          such that  
                            
                               v 
                               ≼ 
                               w 
                            
                         , but  
                            
                               τ 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  q 
                                  , 
                                  v 
                                  ) 
                               
                            
                          and  
                            
                               τ 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  q 
                                  , 
                                  w 
                                  ) 
                               
                            
                          are incomparable subsets.

Axiom (A3) is less intuitive and deserves proper explanations. Suppose an individual possesses all the attributes in the set  
                            
                               C 
                               ⊆ 
                               A 
                            
                         , and the aim is to establish what responses in  
                            V 
                          will be provided by such an individual to the items in  
                            Q 
                          (if random error is excluded). According to a “conjunctive principle”, a trivial way would be to select all and only those item–response pairs  
                            
                               
                                  ( 
                                  q 
                                  , 
                                  v 
                                  ) 
                               
                               ∈ 
                               P 
                            
                          such that  
                            
                               τ 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  q 
                                  , 
                                  v 
                                  ) 
                               
                               ⊆ 
                               C 
                            
                         . This last condition is an immediate generalization of the dichotomous conjunctive rule to the polytomous case. However, this trivial generalization cannot avoid situations where more than a single pair  
                            
                               ( 
                               q 
                               , 
                               v 
                               ) 
                            
                          is selected for the same item  
                            q 
                         . That is, the response to  
                            q 
                          is not unique. Under a deterministic perspective, this kind of non-uniqueness is not tolerated. Axiom (A3) is a precondition to uniqueness. If (i) an entire set  
                            F 
                          of response values satisfies the “trivial” conjunctive rule for item  
                            q 
                          and (ii) there is a response  
                            x 
                          for  
                            q 
                          which is “above” all those in  
                            F 
                          (with respect to the order relation  
                            ≼ 
                         ), then  
                            x 
                          must also satisfy the conjunctive rule.

Finally, Axiom (A4) requires the existence of an upper bound for the subset  
                            F 
                          of response values whenever  
                            
                               τ 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  q 
                                  , 
                                  F 
                                  ) 
                               
                            
                          is a subset of  
                            
                               τ 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  r 
                                  , 
                                  v 
                                  ) 
                               
                            
                          for two items  
                            q 
                          and  
                            r 
                          and a response value  
                            
                               v 
                               ∈ 
                               P 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  r 
                                  ) 
                               
                            
                         .

The following lemma provides a justification to the name “least element” assigned to the response category  
                            z 
                          in  Definition 5 . Moreover, it shows that  
                            z 
                          is “unique” in a precise sense.

Lemma 1 
                            
                               In an attribute map

                                     τ

                               then  
                               
                                  
                                     v 
                                     = 
                                     z 
                                  
                               
                               .

In an attribute map  
                               
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     P 
                                     , 
                                     A 
                                     ,

                                     )

                                        )

                               .

                            (i) From (A1) we have

                                        )

The standard notion of a competence structure ( Heller, Ünlü, & Albert, 2013 ) is extended here, in order to include also applications like that in  Stefanutti, de Chiusole, Gondan, and Maurer (2020) , where the competence structure misses a top element. The generalized notion takes the name of an “attribute structure”.

Definition 5 
                            An  attribute structure  is a pair

                                     , 
                                     C 
                                     )

                                     C 
                                     ⊆

                                     C 
                                     = 
                                     A

                                     , 
                                     C 
                                     )

Attribute maps are based on dichotomous attributes in  
                            A 
                         , and individuals are represented by subsets  
                            
                               C 
                               ⊆ 
                               A 
                            
                          of attributes, named “attribute states”. The aim of an attribute map is to provide a set of rules for establishing, in a deterministic manner, what admissible response will be provided to an item  
                            q 
                          by an individual whose attribute state is  
                            C 
                         . The task is accomplished in a consistent way by the attribute map if there is a sort of compatibility between  
                            C 
                          and the attribute map itself. In this sense, not all subsets of  
                            A 
                          are “compatible” with the attribute map, and this means that the collection of “compatible” attribute states may be a strict subset of the powerset  
                            
                               
                                  2 
                               
                               
                                  A 
                               
                            
                         .

Definition 8  is about completeness in the sense that every response value  
                            v 
                          that is admissible for an item must be observable. Condition  8 (i) is obvious in this sense but it is not sufficient. The response value  
                            v 
                          must also be maximal among all response values satisfying  8 (i). This is what condition  8 (ii) guarantees.

In the first place we have the following Lemma.

The attribute map assigns subsets of attributes to item–response pairs. A  conjunctive interpretation  of such an assignment is that, if  
                            
                               τ 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  q 
                                  , 
                                  v 
                                  ) 
                               
                               = 
                               T 
                            
                          for some nonempty  
                            
                               T 
                               ⊆ 
                               A 
                            
                          then “at least” response  
                            v 
                          is observed for item  
                            q 
                          if the respondent possesses all of the attributes in  
                            T 
                         . The next definition implements a response mechanism which is consistent with this interpretation. Such a response behavior is deterministic and is provided by a special function, named the “conjunctive item–response function”.

                                        C

                                contains eight distinct states ( Fig. 2 ).

In this section, three distinct cases are exemplified, that are named the “quasi-nominal case”, the “partial order case” and the “linear order case”. They differ for the properties of the set  
                         V 
                       of response values. It should be observed that, in the “quasi-nominal case”, all but one values are pairwise incomparable with respect to the order relation, and there is a least element.

A concrete example of a quasi-nominal set of response values can be taken from the recent work by  Stefanutti, de Chiusole, Gondan, and Maurer (2020)  about the modeling of misconceptions in KST. A misconception can be viewed as a “wrong rule” applied in place of the right one (i.e., a skill) in the solution of a problem. Under this approach, the polytomous attribute map assigns to each item–response pair a nonempty subset containing only skills, only misconceptions, or both. More in detail, each problem of a domain is characterized by two or more response categories. One of them corresponds to the correct answer (resulting from applying one or more skills). Another corresponds to a wrong answer due to unknown reasons, and all the other categories correspond to some wrong answer resulting from the application of one or more misconceptions.

A concrete example of a linearly ordered set of response levels is the Likert scale, which is typically used in psychological assessment. There are several types of Likert scale, one of them is the 4-point Likert scale used in the Beck Anxiety Inventory (BAI;  Beck, Epstein, Brown, & Steer, 1993 ). In this test individuals are asked to answer by reporting to what extent each one of the listed anxiety manifestations bothered them during the last week. Respondents must use the following numerical scale with related labels:  
                            
                               0 
                               = 
                            
                          “Not At All”,  
                            
                               1 
                               = 
                            
                          “Mildly, but it didn’t bother me much”,  
                            
                               2 
                               = 
                            
                          “Moderately - it wasn’t pleasant at times”,  
                            
                               3 
                               = 
                            
                          “Severely - it bothered me a lot”. In this test, the levels of the scale are assumed to be linearly ordered.

Let us consider the following set  
                            
                               Q 
                               = 
                               
                                  { 
                                  
                                     
                                        q 
                                     
                                     
                                        1 
                                     
                                  
                                  , 
                                  
                                     
                                        q 
                                     
                                     
                                        2 
                                     
                                  
                                  , 
                                  
                                     
                                        q 
                                     
                                     
                                        3 
                                     
                                  
                                  } 
                               
                            
                          of three items of the BAI. Let item  
                            
                               
                                  q 
                               
                               
                                  1 
                               
                            
                          be “Scared”, item  
                            
                               
                                  q 
                               
                               
                                  2 
                               
                            
                          be “Terrified or afraid”, and item  
                            
                               
                                  q

In this article, polytomous KST has been extended to consider the latent attributes underlying the responses to polytomous items. A deterministic relationship between the available attributes and the observable item responses has been established by means of two mappings denoted as attribute map and item–response function. The attribute map assigns to each item–response pair the set of attributes that are instrumental for observing that particular response to the item. The item–response function assigns to each set of attributes the set of item responses that, according to the attribute map, can be obtained with those attributes. The proposed approach has been shown to be rather general and capable of handling a multitude of polytomous items that can be encountered in practice. Examples have been provided that cover the analysis of responses on Likert scales, responses awarded partial credits, and the investigation of misconceptions.

In an attempt to construct a coherent deterministic relationship between available attributes and observable responses to polytomous items, issues arise that are not encountered in the dichotomous case. This article established the necessary and sufficient conditions that guarantee that (i) for any attribute map there is some attribute structure that is compatible with it ( Theorem 1 ), (ii) the attributes included in any attribute state determine a unique response value for each item ( Theorem 3 ), and (iii) all response values that are admissible for a given item are also observable ( Theorem 5 ).

This article introduced the theoretical framework for an attribute-based extension of polytomous KST, where the items are polytomous and the attributes are, essentially, dichotomous. Moreover, it opens up a whole research agenda where notions and results that have been established in the dichotomous case are extended to the polytomous case. A few of them follow. First of all, since on the part of the attributes the theory is dichotomous, a thorough extension to polytomous attributes is needed. Moreover, the conditions under which the item–response function is injective must be identified, being this a necessary requirement for uniquely assessing the attribute state of any individual from the responses of this individual to the items. To this end, a possible starting point could be the work conducted by  Heller et al. (2015)  and  Heller, Anselmi, Stefanutti, and Robusto (2017)  in the dichotomous case. A disjunctive interpretation of the attribute map could be defined, as well as the possibility of establishing a duality between the conjunctive and disjunctive interpretations. The concept of an attribute map could be extended to include alternative sets of attributes that lead to the same item–response. This would correspond to a generalization of the competency model to polytomous items. The attribute map is a theoretical model of the relationship between available attributes and observable item responses. As such, it needs to be validated empirically. A possibility in this direction is to validate the polytomous structure induced by the attribute map by means of the polytomous local independence model (PoLIM) recently introduced by  Stefanutti, de Chiusole, et al. (2020) . This model is a generalization of the basic local independence model ( Falmagne & Doignon, 1988a ) to polytomous items, and it is flexible enough to handle polytomous items with different structures (e.g., polytomous items with unordered, partially ordered, and totally ordered response categories). Another possibility is to develop an extension of the PoLIM that takes into account the attributes responsible for the item responses. Such a model is expected to be useful in those educational and psychological assessments that aim to infer the attribute state of an individual from the responses of this individual to the items.
